###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OO and DLLs

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "The COBOL Frog" <H.Klink@IMN.NotThisPart.nl>
- **Date:** 2000-04-27T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ecvud$ps6$2@porthos.nl.uu.net>`
- **References:** `<38FD146B.E7EDBAB9@home.com> <20000420233202.03300.00001786@nso-bd.aol.com> <390063BD.58747338@cusys.edu> <3905d92f.10154557@news.cox-internet.com> <3906EEEC.31D439D0@iinet.net.au> <3906F2A2.FF4284E8@cusys.edu> <y0DN4.441$Qe.8116@typhoon.austin.rr.com> <3907b426.1366936@news.cox-internet.com> <fSVN4.960$CP2.9280@typhoon.austin.rr.com> <39087369.59181368@wingate> <4u%N4.2383$ye2.10691@typhoon.austin.rr.com>`

```
If one flavor of OO-COBOL, say from Fujitsu, is able to produce a DLL from one or more classes, and another flavor, say Merant, is able too, than those DLL's can be used in each other run-time-environment, PROVIDED THAT:

- Linkage conventions are exactly the same,
- Memory management (Garbage Collection) works the same
- and thousand of more RTE things work the same.

Probably if Merant and Fujitsu build one compiler together .... pink glasses.

In practice, one builds some reusable component, composed from one or, probably, more than one classes. This component runtime-behavior is described functionally, the needed interface to "talk" with the component is described technically and no source is released, nor is there a dependency like mentioned above.

The descriptions named involve ...

* IDL - Interface Definition Language
* A Commonly usable Object Request Broker Architecture to provide for a vendor-independent inter-object/inter-component linkage

This is just an extra layer to HIDE the complexities of runtime-tied-multi-vendor-components. COM or CORBA and things like that are a logical next step.

Nice thing is that a COM/CORBA component in no way needs to be built OO.

the OO frog
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Project control and OO

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** khansen@screenio.com (Kevin Hansen)
- **Date:** 2000-05-04T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3905df51.4377254@wingate>`
- **References:** `<38FD146B.E7EDBAB9@home.com> <20000420233202.03300.00001786@nso-bd.aol.com> <390063BD.58747338@cusys.edu> <3905d92f.10154557@news.cox-internet.com>`

```
On Tue, 25 Apr 2000 17:44:58 GMT, thaneH@softwaresimple.com (Thane
Hubbell) wrote:

>On Fri, 21 Apr 2000 08:20:45 -0600, Howard Brazee
><howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>is in Flux and I expect several more changes before final acceptance.

Two observations here...

Structured techniques do not themselves cause blown budgets, and never
have.  Virtually ALL large projects go overbudget (says the guy that
has done lots of large applications, built a couple of houses, and
built a fair-sized sailboat) - virtually all of which went over
budget, sigh...

The problem relates to determining a budget before the scope of the
project is known.  As time passes and targets move, the project scope
typically expands, but the project-control methodology (if there even
IS one) fails to take this into account at the time.    Very few
project managers are willing to allocate sufficient resources to
project control; it takes a LOT to fully understand and track a large
project, and to keep the numbers and expectations up-to-date.

As for OO, Thane, you're absolutely correct.  Until there's a standard
we can get our arms around, and products that implement it, it's all
just words...

Now, if adoption of OO forces project management to be more realistic,
that would not be a bad thing.  In the large projects I've observed,
much of the time the management sets the budget first and the goal
next.  

I well remember sitting in the office of a certain upper-management
State bureaucratic-type 20 years ago, presenting my very careful
analysis of the computing hardware needed by the State of Alaska in
preparation for a major procurement.  We needed a pair of 3033's,
conservatively.  The director said, and I quote, "The legislature will
never approve that budget; revise your numbers and recommend a pair of
3031's."  (MUCH smaller machines).

Funny thing was, we had a pair of 3033's a couple of years later and
they were completely saturated almost immediately, just as forecast.

Anyway, this sort of management arrogance is probably the root cause
of many over-budget projects.

Really makes you feel as though it's pointless to do a good job.  I
went off on my own shortly thereafter and never looked back.  

Kevin
NORCOM COBOL Programming Tools
GUI ScreenIO; a cool COBOL/Windows Screen Manager
http://www.ScreenIO.com
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Project control and OO

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au>
- **Date:** 2000-05-05T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3912304A.ADF4AC96@zip.com.au>`
- **References:** `<38FD146B.E7EDBAB9@home.com> <20000420233202.03300.00001786@nso-bd.aol.com> <390063BD.58747338@cusys.edu> <3905d92f.10154557@news.cox-internet.com> <3905df51.4377254@wingate>`

```
Kevin Hansen wrote:
> 
> Now, if adoption of OO forces project management to be more
> realistic, that would not be a bad thing.  In the large projects I've
> observed, much of the time the management sets the budget first and
> the goal next.

There are two OOs:  OOP and OOD.  We discuss object oriented
programming on this forum, little has been said about object orient
design.  They must go hand in hand to make the whole process sing.
Some of the object modelling in UML will allow for better
visualization and communication of the requirements, this would assist
the correct estimate and better management.  The manager is
responsible for getting this right however, my company is looking into
'Prince2' which focusses on project management.

The software development process is about a total synergy of
processes. Programmers tend to focus at too low a level to understand
that synergy.  We want to train software 'engineers' not better
programmers.

(Engineers is a debatable word subject to legal requirements, there is
no better word to describe it at this time though).

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

I don't know about the rest of you, but I have been a programmer for thirty
years now, and still am.  That is what my business cards say: Programmer.

Even in my own company, I get hassled over it.  I get little boys fresh out
of their MBA that want to make me a "systems engineer", or a "vice president
of software".  Even, heaven forbid, a "systems analyst". I debate,
occasionally, telling them to place "BOSS" on their letterhead, and then to
read it twice a day.

Thane and Ken, you should be ashamed of yourselves. You are being suckered.

"The software development process is about a total synergy of
processes. Programmers tend to focus at too low a level to    understand
that synergy.  We want to train software 'engineers' not better
programmers."

After you tell me what that means, tell me about how the person that wrote
it can simplify things to the point a computer can understand them.  After
that, ask yourself if he really can program better than every "programmer"
in the world.

Einstein once said "An expert can explain his subject to an intelligent five
year old".  To explain things to a computer, I do not need to be called an
expert at focussing on the total synergy of processes.  If I wanted to be
that, I would have cards printed with "bullshit artist" on them, and every
intelligent five year old would understand perfectly.

Can you imagine Shakespeare being ashamed of being a writer? If he were
still alive, we would have bullshit artists wanting to put "plot engineer"
or "theme development analyst" on his business card.  I'll let them play
about with making taupe screens with chartreuse backgrounds while I
concentrate on making things work.

Thank god it is Friday.

Donald

competence.  A vendor such as E.D.S. can supply a body and nobody knew

Warren Simmons wrote:
> 
> I remember a one week course I took at UCLA one summer.  There I was
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> before designing how it will be organized on the computer.

Both :-}  I do top down design by faking it, see article by Parnas.
http://chacs.nrl.navy.mil/publications/a7/misc/fake-it

I believe that you tend to try and do it top down, but most designers
also tend to think of the details at the same time (it is not pure top
down).

I also think that there are many methods of designing systems better. 
Some are programmer centric (extreme programming) some are methodology
centric (rapid development by Steve McConnell).  Both are valid
methods, you must be aware of them and make an informed choice as to
your way of working.

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au> wrote:

>The software development process is about a total synergy of
>processes. Programmers tend to focus at too low a level to understand
>that synergy.  We want to train software 'engineers' not better

i agree that more should focus on design than actually requiring oo
programming. designing the system allows you to break up the problem
so if you throw more programmers at it, it can be done on time.

the way i look at alot of systems is, they can be designed at typing
speed and maintained at typing speed / possible sub-programs. in all
cases, the typing speed is the speed at which the code gets developed,
and the design is how many programmers you can throw at the problem at
once.

```

G Moore wrote in message ...
>Ken Foskey <waratah@zip.com.au> wrote:
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Of course.  However, it is all still programming.

I am reminded of a good friend who is one of Canada's premier poets.  A
writer's writer.  When asked "How do I become a professional writer?"
replied "Finish high school". He then went on.

"Anybody damn fool that can finish high school can write. Then do it for ten
years.  By the end of ten years you will realize that writing is nothing.
Deciding what to write is the difficult thing".

Of course deciding what to program and how to program it is part of
programming. Simply being able to speak the language does not make anybody a
"writer" in any language, including Cobol. Neither does being able to type
it in.  However, it also does not make it "systems engineering".  It is an
intregal part of programming.

If you cannot write it down in a language that a computer can understand,
then you have not "programmed" anything.  And if you have written it down,
and it does work, then you are a programmer.  After that, it is just a
matter of Vocabulary.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ UML was Re: Project control and OO

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2000-05-11T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<391B5ABC.934A9C21@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<38FD146B.E7EDBAB9@home.com> <20000420233202.03300.00001786@nso-bd.aol.com> <390063BD.58747338@cusys.edu> <3905d92f.10154557@news.cox-internet.com> <3905df51.4377254@wingate> <3912304A.ADF4AC96@zip.com.au>`

```
Should the Universal Modeling Language generate the final code?  If it
doesn't, how much effort is needed to make sure the programs don't drift
from the UML specification?  I would like to see higher level tools than
COBOL and I understand that they exist.  Texas Instruments sold one of
the higher lever languages that I understand could generate either C or
COBOL code.  I also have heard that the Oracle application language
generates COBOL code.  

Repositories will be of major benefit in the future but will require a
lot of discipline if they are to work.  I think that they may force the
qualification issue for COBOL since you would want to have only one
definition of customer number in the repository regardless of the number
of database tables or record descriptions in which it appears.

Clark Morris, CFM Technical Programming Services Inc., cfmtech@istar.ca

"Clark F. Morris, Jr." wrote:
> 
> Should the Universal Modeling Language generate the final code?

No, it is a design tool.  It could build the fundamental structure, if
there was a market I am sure Rose would add a language module to their
tool. It currently does C++, Java and others.

>  If it doesn't, how much effort is needed to make sure the programs
> don't drift from the UML specification?

Do we stop writing business requirements because they do not reflect
the eventual result?

It is a design tool and it is very descriptive to the end users at the
first level.  I have found the diagrams are excellent at portraying a
system in a number of different views of the design quickly and
easily.

Rose does 'round trip' engineering of C++ code, it reanalyses the
actual code and rebuilds the model on the actual implementation.  THis
is not that useful unless the drift is significant and even then it
apparently does not work that well.  It can however generate a
starting model for rework on existing code, this is useful.

> I would like to see higher level tools than COBOL and I understand
> that they exist.  Texas Instruments sold one of the higher lever
> languages that I understand could generate either C or COBOL code.  I
> also have heard that the Oracle application language generates COBOL
> code.

I am not a fan of these.  If the language is broken then get a better
language. Components (free or purchased) will ultimately give us much
better use that code generators.  When the code generator has a bug
you must regenerate the entire code, with OO isolation you simply
repair the object that implements the code and everything else is
fixed automatically (OK on relink).
> 
> Repositories will be of major benefit in the future but will require
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> number of database tables or record descriptions in which it appears.

You are now talking data (object) dictionaries. A repository is the
definition of an object, yes the problem is to find the objects and
this does require a good dictionary, to date I have not seen a good
answer to this question.

At least with a repository you do have a centralized storage of all
the names to look up, next we need to organize them.

The other problem not yet covered by the standard is name clashes.  If
I use Fujitsu compiler and buried inside their implementation of
window handling is class 'fred' and then I buy a source component from
a third party (say oracle) and they also define class 'fred' then how
do we make these names unique.  This is where C++ 'name spaces' help,
you say this object is defined in this name space.  If you need to
clarify which particular object you mean you add the name space
qualifier.

Thanks
Ken Foskey
http://www.zipworld.com.au/~waratah/
```

Windows has some elements of OO.  The device drivers give us a clear
interface that allows multiple devices to be used transparently to the
application.

The programming interface to windows is through a massive switch
statement.  This is definitely not OO like, NEXT is a better example
and it publishes the OO interface and you inherit from it.

Try an explain call backs and hooks to someone new.  This is one of
the concepts of OO that is a little difficult to grasp.  This concept
works 100% easier in OO than procedural code.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

- **From:** "donald tees" <donald@willmack.com>
- **Date:** 2000-04-18T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8digrn$lv7$1@news.igs.net>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco>`

```
Judson, I think that you have been turned off by the typical OOP project
where someone inherits a slew of objects they do not understand and try to
design a system using them.

The OOP methodology, ignoring the actual object that one uses for the
moment, is far more useful and versatile than the subroutine.  It adds two
entirely new bits of functionality to the subroutine --- rigorous compile
time argument checking, and the ability to use multiple instances of the
same routine.

That latter is far more important than it may seem at first glance. If you
have, for example, a screen that is managed by a subroutine, then you can
only open one screen at a time with it, or it must contain a tables for
multiple screens, or you have to dupe it under a different name for a second
screen.  The ability to create, at the mainline level, a number of instances
of the subroutine and simply call/invoke different instances to get multiple
screens is crucially important for windows, for example.  Similar conundrums
abound, and the Cobol lack in that area has held Cobol back from being very
good for bottom up tool development.

I think if you forgot all about OOP as a methodology, and simply started to
play with it as a replacement for the subroutine, you would start thinking
very differently. OOP does *not* mean canned libraries. It is a better way
of organising existing libraries.




Judson McClendon wrote in message ...

Clark F. Morris, Jr. wrote in message <38FCF59C.7DD17740@istar.ca>...
>Based on the posting below, I have the following questions:
>
>1.  How can you have rigorous compile time argument checking for
>separately compiled objects?  Is there also an upon invocation checking
>done for separately compiled objects?

Compiling an object produces a REP file, which is the symbol table for the
properties.  That is used by the compiler to compile main programs that use
the objects.  The REPOSITORY paragraph tells the compiler where to look for
that file. Once the repository is established, the prerequisite symbol table
of the objects is merged with the symbol table of the program being
compiled.  Moves in and out of objects are checked exactly the same as if
the symbol table was local, execpt that you need to reference them to the
object.  A method, for example, that requires
three arguements of picture 99 that is called with other that three
arguements will cause a compile time error, as will using a picture 999.

>
>2.  How do you distinguish between the instances of an object and choose
>the correct one when there are multiple instances?

Well, you set them up, so you have to keep track of which is which. That is
done by using names, just as with any other cobol variable. I could create
two objects called SCREEN1 and SCREEN2, or I could create two objects called
ERROR-SCREEN and MENU-SCREEN.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Checking of ability to use requested object wasRe: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2000-04-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3903920F.2EA5919A@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco> <8digrn$lv7$1@news.igs.net> <38FCF59C.7DD17740@istar.ca> <38fdc7de.10621861@news.cox-internet.com>`

```
The repository function and its operation described below sounds very
much like the repository function requested by the Guide/SHARE Language
Futures Task Force Final Report issued in 1984.  The difference between
what we (I was one of the minor participants) were requesting and what
you are talking about is that we wanted this checking done for the
standard call interfaces.  This included detection of mismatches and
possible handling of them if possible, requested and appropriate
(display numeric to fixed binary or a change of parameter calling
sequence from that of Fortran to that of PL/1 for example).

Clark Morris, CFM Technical Programming Services Inc., cfmtech@istar.ca

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 2000-04-19T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38FE6D7F.2130@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco> <8digrn$lv7$1@news.igs.net>`

```
donald tees wrote:
> 
> Judson, I think that you have been turned off by the typical OOP project
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> same routine.

The former bit of functionality -- compile-time argument checking --
is indeed is a valuable addition, but it has nothing to do with OO
methodology.  For example, C and PL/SQL both offer it (though it's
sort of optional for C), and neither of those languages is object 
oriented.  It is an accident of history that compile-time argument 
checking was brought to COBOL by dialects which also brought OO 
extensions.

It's not clear what you mean by the latter: the ability to use
multiple instances of the same routine.  If you are referring to the
ability to dynamically allocate multiple instances of the same kind
of data element, that ability also has nothing to do with OO
methodology.  You can do that in C, PL/SQL, Pascal, and many other
languages.  You can even do it in COBOL, depending on the dialect,
without reference to the OO extensions.  All you need is pointers
and some way to allocate memory.

On the other hand if you are referring to polymorphism -- the ability
to bind the same procedure name to multiple different routines,
depending on the dynamic type of an object -- then you're right.
Polymorphism is not only a useful technique (at least occasionally), 
but also a cornerstone of the OO paradigm.

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

donald tees wrote:
> 
> Judson, I think that you have been turned off by the typical OOP project
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Can't let this one fly!
Operating systems have been doing this for YEARS before OO was ever
thought of.
You don't need OO to re-invoke your subroutine passing parms, ONE OF
WHICH is the
anchor for the "object" being acted upon.

I don't have a problem with the concept of OO.
I have a problem with the implementation.

I am not convinced that the heirarchy of classes was a good idea.
My impression of programming in OO, is that you are continually
searching the
classes for a object and/or a method to use. I am slowly forming the opinion
that the burden of scanning for objects/methods is eventually going to
be so great that it will become the bottleneck in the OO implementation.
I suspect that the OO concept was applied to too low a abstraction
layer. i.e. print a string, one has to override the current print method
of the string with a new method pointing to a new object "printer".

This tends to break the project into a set of disparate methods in
multiple object and classes.
There is no coherent WHOLE to which I can point and say that here is
your application.

Furthermore, there is nothing that you can do with OO, that I can't do
with subroutines or subprograms.
I can have the subroutine/program own its data and publish an interface
standard. This is basically OO, without the hype and needless complexity
of the OO class library.

I think the concept of REUSE has been hijacked by OO to mean "YOU MUST
AND WILL REUSE EVERYTHING".
Does it really make sense to make a String an object? a Character?
```

Joseph J Katnic wrote:
> 
> I am not convinced that the heirarchy of classes was a good idea.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> of the string with a new method pointing to a new object "printer".

I am picturing a mainframe OO shop:

I have to get my program out - I'm pretty sure there are objects
available which I can use for my program, but I really don't want to
pore through documentation to find out if they can be adapted for my
purposes.  So I create my own object.  A couple of months later, I need
to modify my program.  I see that I can change the object to make my
modification.  Fortunately, nobody else is using my object (I don't know
about that guy who is just about to implement his new program using my
object, so I go ahead and change it. 

OOps, I had better keep my objects away from other people so that I can
still own them, once they go into the common library it would take an
act of Congress to change them (rightfully so - you don't want to be
breaking other people's code).


How many people have compiled working programs just so that you can do a
search for key fields in the listing?  (looking through the source won't
work with your COPY and COPY REPLACING statements).  The more you spread
your program out, the harder it will be to do maintenance.   That is of
course, unless the academia are right - that once you create an object,
it will always do what you expect in the way you expect, that your code
will simply be a bunch of plugged in objects, which are guaranteed to
work correctly.
```

```


Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> Joseph J Katnic wrote:
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
> work correctly.

Valid comments Howard. Rather like you've got a DBA for DBs, in a medium
to big shop it would seem you have got to have an OOA (administrator);
might be the same job as the DBA. For the moment ignoring vendor
supplied classes, it boils down to establishing in-house standards of
what you perceive as your immediate needs - say for example you hit upon
the idea of file-handling (flat or DB). You determine your file needs
and write the in-house classes. Now THEY DO NOT CHANGE unless the OOA
sanctions the changes. This doesn't stultify the development of your
classes. You have a need which is a 'slight twist' on what is available.
The OOA sanctions this new-method which either (a) merely invokes others
already existing to give you the result you want, or (b) an entirely new
method because your requirements are entirely different. Frankly if it
is planned properly at the beginning I doubt that you would really need
option (b).

That's very broad I know - but I think you'll appreciate it gives you
reasonable surety plus adaptability - PROVIDING it is controlled by the
OOA.

Jimmy
```

"James J. Gavan" wrote:

> That's very broad I know - but I think you'll appreciate it gives you
> reasonable surety plus adaptability - PROVIDING it is controlled by the
> OOA.

That makes sense.  The object repository has to be taken care of the way
a database does.  This makes it easy for individual programmers to move
into OO - they don't need DBAs either.  But a large shop WILL have
changes and design issues which need to be approved by people who really
understand the tools.

It does make it harder to get going though.  It might happen when a
company buys a package such as an ERP, complete with datase and OO
repository (whatever it is called - or more likely whatever marketing
people decide to call THEIR special mainframe size libraries with
cross-reference tools).  They train the DBA and OOA as well as providing
OO training for the programmers and analysts.

It could make or break a vendor.

On Tue, 18 Apr 2000 13:07:03 -0500, "Judson McClendon"

>> could code using this.".  We need OO to attract users of other
>> languages to COBOL.

>will come because it offers them something better in one or more ways.

I agree with the last.  However, I disagree with the premise.  You
see, many of the people entering the workforce now have NEVER had an
education in a procedural language.  They had OO from the start.  That
makes a procedural language as unfamiliar and scary to them as OO is
to the procedural programmer.  For the OO educated programmer the
procedural style and vocabulary is as hard to grasp as OO style and
vocabulary for the procedural programmer.  Having OO in COBOL opens
the door for its use, with the benefits that COBOL offers over other
languages, to these OO educated programmers.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com>

Thane, I agree with you.

Languages become irrelevant when their use drops below a
critical level. Adding OO and other features will help to extend
the life of COBOL by keeping it relevant.

The local community college teaches VB and C++; but when
they advertised for a programmer they needed COBOL. How
long will it be before the business office drops COBOL because
the college is not teaching it anymore? OO COBOL may spark
a renewed interest among academics.

There is no reason why one could not and should not limit their
use of COBOL to the existing features; using the enhancements
when necessary and understood.
------------------
Rick Smith

…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> when necessary and understood.

I am dubious that the academic community would ever embrace COBOL,
no matter what was done to it.  Back when COBOL was the only serious
language for business programming, and by far the most used language
in the world, the academic community spurned it, and only taught it
at all because of pressure from business.  "It wasn't invented here."
Instead, they embraced languages like Pascal, which not only were a
flash in the pan compared to the widespread use of COBOL, but weren't
suitable for business use anyway.  Academia, it seems, despises the
hand (business) that feeds it.  Too plebeian and practical.  If it
isn't intellectually interesting, it is useless, in their eyes.
```

Judson McClendon wrote in message ...
>Rick Smith wrote:
[...]
>> [...] OO COBOL may spark
>> a renewed interest among academics.
[...]
>I am dubious that the academic community would ever embrace COBOL,
>no matter what was done to it.  Back when COBOL was the only serious
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>isn't intellectually interesting, it is useless, in their eyes.

To paraphase a quote I once read, "Never attribute to conspiracy
that for which there is a logical explanation."

For the most part, the part of the academic community that rejects
COBOL is Computer Science. Is RPG taught in Computer Science?
Need we resort to "Not Invented Here" as a reason? No! COBOL,
RPG, and many other languages are, simply, poor choices for teaching
Computer Science.

FORTRAN was used initially because computers were install in
the College of Mathmatics. ALGOL replaced FORTRAN because
it added structure. Pascal replaced ALGOL because it has
structure and is easier to learn. C replaced Pascal because it
allowed separate compilation. C++ replaced C because OO is
superior for some CS problems. Some other languages are
taught because of their special nature. COBOL never had much
to offer CS until now. Do I think COBOL will replace C++ in
CS departments. No, but I think the new features might allow
some to consider a class in COBOL due to its special nature.

If academics are no longer damning COBOL, university and
college administrations might be more amenable to teaching
COBOL. It may be a "long shot" but without OO there is no
chance at all.
------------------
Rick Smith

"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com> wrote in message news:8dn490
> >at all because of pressure from business.  "It wasn't invented here."
> >Instead, they embraced languages like Pascal, which not only were a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

While it is true that the Academic society tend to behave like you describe
above, I would like to emphasize that the intention of Niclaus Wirth in the
creation of Pascal was to help students think structured.

One may say many nice things about Cobol, unfortunately, encouragement to
structured programming is not one of them. In that context, Pascal had its
mission.

That Mr Wirth had true objectives in this sense, is in my opinion proven by
his successors Modula-2 and eventually Oberon, which both in their steps has
brought further his care for structured and modularised programming.

> COBOL,
> RPG, and many other languages are, simply, poor choices for teaching
> Computer Science.

While other languages proves as a better platform for understanding certain
issues, this does not imply that particular languages are totally unusable
for the purpose. Less suitable yes, but nevertheless suitable.

> it added structure. Pascal replaced ALGOL because it has
> structure and is easier to learn. C replaced Pascal because it
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> some to consider a class in COBOL due to its special nature.

This is truly a simplification of history. Pascal for instance, was never
made with the aim to replace Algol, it is a real born part of the Algol
family yes. But it was not invented to reprace Algol. As a matter of fact,
Niclaus Wirth invented Pascal because he considered Basic less suitable.

The same thing counts for C vs Pascal. C was never made to replac Pascal,
nor was C++ meant to replace C.

Whether  a language replaces another is a question about market penetration.
In this context we are facing pure Darwinism. Survival of the fittest.
Obviously Cobol has proven an exception to Darwinism as Cobol still isn't
(at least not the standard) up to date on the hottest. Or, one may say that
the Darwinism is proven as the computing environment has divided into
various segments, each with its own requirements and conditions, just like
there are Camels in the desert and polar bears in the arctic.

Eventually, some languages did not keep up, nor did they adopt to a special
environment, then they more or less died out. May be still being kept alive
some place by special interest group. Like some sort of computer
environmentalist group :-)

This turned out to be a really interesting issue though...

> If academics are no longer damning COBOL, university and
> college administrations might be more amenable to teaching
> COBOL. It may be a "long shot" but without OO there is no
> chance at all.

Have anyone out there ever thought of the fact that you may get a Java IDE,
VB IDE, Delphi IDE, C/C++ IDE for change compared to what the cost is of a
Cobol IDE. Well, it is true that the recent years this has changed, but it
used to be like this. If not the only reason, this is a reason with huge
impact of why Cobol is not more widespread than it is. Remember Universities
/ Schools have limited resources, when they can have something for free,
both for themselves and the students, it helps a lot on the decision.

To see this in a historical perspective, consider Borland Delphi, which is a
wide sub set of the original pascal invented by Niclaus Wirth. Pascal was
almost used in educational environments only until The Danish company
Polysoft launched their PolyPascal for change back in the early 80s. Philipe
Khan at Borland saw this as a winner, and bought it all, including the
brilliant Dane behind. Needless to say, the renamed product Turbo Pascal
turned Borland into a multi million dollar company and is basically the only
reason Pascal still is widely known today (my opinion). The essence here is
what Kahn did back then, he saw the power of a development utility that
everyone could afford.

What wouldn't Cobol had been today with the same move back then...

Unfortunately, although I regret to say so, I also believe that the ANSI
standards has been drag to take into consideration of this. Standards are
both beneficial and disadvantageous. Sometimes good, sometimes bad.

Everybody have a nice day. I have spoken way too much already.

Cheesle

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

- **From:** "Michael C. Kasten" <mck9@swbell.net>
- **Date:** 2000-04-19T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38FE79E6.6EE9@swbell.net>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco>`

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> Thane Hubbell wrote:
> >
> > 4.  OO COBOL has not been widely accepted as good or necessary,
> > especially in the mainframe world.  

[snip]

> >  Most IBM mainframers don't use and don't care about OO COBOL.

The lack of enthusiasm among mainframers may have less to do with 
OO COBOL than with IBM's implementation of it.  On my website I have 
argued that IBM's version of OO COBOL is so inflexible as to be
almost unusable.  It also requires mastery of a complex and cumbersome 
infrastructure.  Other dialects of OO COBOL probably have more to
offer, but I haven't had occasion to become familiar with them.

[snip; Judson argues that the the benefits of object-oriented
 methodology are of doubtful value, when weighed agains the 
 associated complexity]

If the OO paradigm is useful at all, it's presumably more useful in
some situations than in others.  It's worth asking the question: in
what kinds of situation is the OO paradigm most likely to be useful?

It's even more important to ask: when is it *least* likely to be 
useful?

I have addressed these questions at some length on my website.  See:

	http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/ooc/whenoo.html

[I posted a pointer to the same page some time ago, but a number of
new faces have joined the group since then.]

I'm not sure whether I believe everything I wrote on that page, but
perhaps it will spark some useful discussion.  In summary, the OO 
paradigm is most likely to be useful when:

1. The requirements are stable and well-understood;

2. The requirements are largely under the control of the developers
and can therefore be tailored to the technology;

3. There is a natural and intuitively appealing use for polymorphism.

(The first two are helpful whether you're using OO methodology or
not.  However they're especially important for an OO system because
of the need to get the class design right at the outset.)

These conditions largely apply to wholly artificial worlds such as a
GUI or a computer game.  They generally don't apply very well to the 
kinds of applications for which COBOL is most typically used.  We are 
routinely expected to implement business rules which are arbitrary, 
weirdly complex, and constantly changing.

Consequently, I suspect that OO COBOL will be more useful for 
implementing internal abstractions than for modelling business entities.

For those who have been actually using OO COBOL, rather than just
toying with it, as I have done: Where has the OO paradigm worked?
Where has it failed?  In future projects, where would you expect it to
be most (and least) successful?

Michael C. Kasten	mck9@swbell.net
http://home.swbell.net/mck9/cobol/cobol.html
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com>
- **Date:** 2000-04-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8dmvq4$171t$1@news.hitter.net>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco> <38FE79E6.6EE9@swbell.net> <38FF2226.A0BE2E48@cusys.edu>`

```

Howard Brazee wrote in message <38FF2226.A0BE2E48@cusys.edu>...
>"Michael C. Kasten" wrote:
[...]
>> 3. There is a natural and intuitively appealing use for polymorphism.
>
>This is why people think GUI with OO.  It is not nearly as obvious that
>it applies to data processing.

invoke clients "get-this-one"
    using client-key returning this-client

invoke products "get-this-one"
    using product-key returning this-product

invoke orders "get-this-one"
    using order-key returning this-order

"get-this-one" is a polymophic method that applies to a
keyed collection of objects that have a primary key.

invoke clients "get-next" returning this-client

invoke transactions "get-next" returning this-transaction

"get-next" is a polymorphic method that applies to any
collection that may be accessed sequentially.

invoke this-transaction "execute"

invoke this-menu-item "execute"

"execute" is a polymorphic method that applies to any
object where the exact nature of the process should not
be described more succinctly.

To find polymophism in data processing, describe the
processes in general terms (or psuedocode). Wherever
the same action applies to more than one class of data
(transactions vs menu-items or unordered vs ordered
data storage) or to different subclass implementations
(linked-list vs sequential file vs SQL cursor), the action
described is polymophic.

Of course ADD, SUBTRACT, MULTIPLY, DIVIDE, and
COMPUTE are polymorphic across the virtual classes:
PACKED-DECIMAL, BINARY, and (numeric) DISPLAY;
but that's a topic for another day.
------------------
Rick Smith
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@aiservices.com>
- **Date:** 2000-04-20T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8dn8p2$1hk2$1@news.hitter.net>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco> <38FE79E6.6EE9@swbell.net> <38FF2226.A0BE2E48@cusys.edu> <8dmvq4$171t$1@news.hitter.net> <38FF3E11.CE705460@cusys.edu>`

```
The code is not obscure, you're just not seeing it clearly! ;-)

Here's the bottom line!

Any form of modular programming, OO or not, requires trust.
You must trust that whatever code you call or invoke will do
exactly what it is supposed to do. Without trust OO will never
work, for you.

You are not a candidate for OO, if you always examine the
assembler output of a program to make sure the compiler
didn't screw up.

You are not a candidate for OO, if you have examined the
assembler for every "CEE-----" subroutine and approve.

You are not a candidate for OO, if you ...

A big part of OO is that you should not see "exactly" what is
happening. What "exactly" is happening is supposed to be
hidden. A "get-next" method, returning an object of some type,
does what it says. Why should it make any difference whether
the implementation is link-list, sequential file, or database
cursor? How does the implementation affect how you use the
object? As the user of an object, you must not depend upon an
implementation which could later be changed.

Yes, having 10 000 lines of code makes it very easy to see
everything that happens in a part of the system; but 600 lines
showing only the relevant parts is easier to work with.

I am not convinced that OO is a cure-all for all system development,
but I do see some of the advantages; and, yes, I do have trust
issues with other code, which is really a problem because I work
alone. :-)
------------------
Rick Smith

Howard Brazee wrote in message <38FF3E11.CE705460@cusys.edu>...
>Now comes the hard part - convincing us of the advantage of the
>following code.  (we can already see disadvantages).
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>> >it applies to data processing.
[...]
>> invoke clients "get-next" returning this-client
>>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> collection that may be accessed sequentially.
```

Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> Now comes the hard part - convincing us of the advantage of the
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> bit obscure, where we can't see exactly what is happening.


I really don't see where this is obscure Howard. Let's expand on one of
Rick's examples :-

> > invoke clients "get-this-one"
> >     using client-key returning this-client

--------------------------------------------------------------------

Global Working-Storage Section.
01 CLIENT-record.
   05 CLIENT-result.
   copy "clientsfile.cpy" replacing ==(tag)== by ==CLIENT==.

*>----------------------------
Method-id. "get-client-data".
*>-----------------------------
Local-storage section.
*>
*> We can put the results in here or up above in Global Working-Storage.
*>
01 ls-values.
   05 ls-result.
   copy "clientsfile.cpy" replacing ==(tag)== by ==ls==.
Linkage section.
01 lnk-client-key.
Procedure Division using lnk-client-key.
  invoke os-ClientsFile "get-this-one"
      using lnk-client-key returning ls-values    *> OR
*>    using lnk-client-key returning CLIENT-record
     
   if ls-result <> zeroes
*> if CLIENT-result <> zeroes
      then we have an error.....

   else................
   End-if

Exit Method
End Method "get-client-data".
*>--------------------------------------------

What's ambiguous about the above - you have the file-search result-flag
and a copy of the Client record either in local-storage  or in W/S ?

Jimmy

"James J. Gavan" wrote:
> 
> Howard Brazee wrote:
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
> Jimmy

Not much obscurity there there.  But this example has a disadvantage of
not offering much either.  We see a different syntax to learn, but
that's no big deal - if we saw the advantage.

Talking with mainframe programmers, one of the biggest complaints is
when we have to use called programs.  We prefer having our code in front
of us, owned by us, and accountable to nobody but us.  When we have to
pull down a called program and compile it to our own libraries with
debug statements, we complain.  When we have to use someone else's
program which someone else obfuscated the return code, we complain.  

So now we see a new technology which makes some promises.  We're not
quite "getting" it, but when someone shows us how a subset of it can
look just like our called programs (with a slight change of syntax), we
are not overwhelmed with enthusiasm.


The obscurity does exist in OO.  Obscure and hidden are synonyms.  OO
development has source spread out much more than what procedural
programmers reluctantly put up with.

My point is that there we will need to be sold on the results.  We will
need to see productivity gains in large maintenance environments.  And
we will be complaining as we move into that environment until the
movement we look at the results and are converted.  (unless we are using
this as a lever into other new technology to improve our resumes)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Multiple Topics - One message -- CA-World discussed too!

- **From:** Joseph J Katnic <josephk@iinet.net.au>
- **Date:** 2000-04-22T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3901A59B.A27A46AB@iinet.net.au>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco> <38FE79E6.6EE9@swbell.net> <38FF2226.A0BE2E48@cusys.edu> <8dmvq4$171t$1@news.hitter.net> <38FF3E11.CE705460@cusys.edu> <3900B54B.3982129D@home.com> <3900C80F.E13C55AC@cusys.edu>`

```

Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> Talking with mainframe programmers, one of the biggest complaints is
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Source code in front of you!
Hmm, try this then.

A large implementation on a mainframe can conceivably contain 100
sub-programs or sub-routines. These subPrograms can be used in many
programs (linked together). There may even be 2 to 3 times the number of
main programs as there are sub programs. So we have 200 to 300 main
programs and 100 sub programs with various linkages.

Assuming all the source is in the same library, you have 300 to 400
source members and quite possibly another 100 copy books for the data
passing to the subroutines/subprograms. This is quite unwieldy, but
manageable, because all of the programs and copy books, are in a set of
libraries that relate to this project. Some of the subprograms may be in
a common library however.

Now how is this different from the following:
Our 300 main programs and 100 sub programs equate to approximately 400
methods (I know there may be many more!). After analysis it is
determined that approximately 5 methods per object will be created. So
we have 80 objects with 5 methods each.

These 80 objects are represented by 80 classes. Each class has to be
placed in the class hierarchy so that it can take advantage of
inheritance. In reality, you would create a new hierarchy branch for
this application, because what you are building makes no use of the
generic methods (other than create/destroy object etc.). I.e. you are
not creating a type of string or character or number. You are creating a
whole new application paradigm.

So we have a new hierarchy branch, with the 80 classes under it,
inheriting via some design some of the 400 methods.
THERE IS NOTHING IN OO to stop you from making ALL of the classes have
the application class as their parent. I.E. they do not inherit from
each other. IF YOU DO THIS you cannot share/inherit code (methods)
between classes.

Now, here's the kicker,
If you have the time to do it, start by NOT inheriting any methods.
Place all of the classes under the application class at the same level.
Now as you code and test, some method duplication will arise. As it
arises and when you have a feel for the generalities of the method MOVE
the classes into a hierarchical arrangement so that you can share the
methods. (This is called Refactorization). Now as the project
progresses, the class hierarchy will EVOLVE into approximately the right
hierarchy for the problem at hand.

When you do maintenance, you must look to see if any methods you are
introducing are duplicated in other classes. If they are, you should
consider refactoring and changing the class hierarchy to take advantage
of code re-use.

So now you have 80 classes, 400 methods and the same problem of browsing
all that text. One advantage you will have is that the Class browser
will show you ALL the objects belonging to the application class - they
are the ones lower in the hierarchy on the same branch.
```

Joseph J Katnic wrote:
> 
> Howard Brazee wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs and 100 sub programs with various linkages.


I have never come across anything like that.  When I said mainframe
programmers complain about called programs, I was thinking of a ratio of
1-100!

Typically a mainframe programmer wants to do a search in the program he
has to maintain.  But these days it isn't that easy.  Do a compile then
do the search to catch all copy members, then search for all instances
of " call " (if our shop has program calls), excluding those created by
the pre-compiler.  If we see a called program which isn't one of the
standards, we repeat until we see everything in the program we are
analyzing.
 
> Assuming all the source is in the same library, you have 300 to 400
> source members and quite possibly another 100 copy books for the data
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> a common library however.

Copybooks make it hard for programmers - but we have been sold on their
advantages.  I presume eventually we will be sold on the advantage of
objects as well - despite expecting the same type of increased
difficulty.
 
> So now you have 80 classes, 400 methods and the same problem of browsing
> all that text. One advantage you will have is that the Class browser
> will show you ALL the objects belonging to the application class - they
> are the ones lower in the hierarchy on the same branch.

I have never done any mainframe OO programing.  I have no idea how hard
it would be to browse a very large system.  Certainly the tools designed
for subroutines and copy libraries are not up to the task.  Neither are
the data dictionaries I have worked with.

Also, mainframes often have very strict version control.  We have to
check out our code, and make sure everybody who uses that code signs off
in our testing before it's migrated into production.  That makes
programmers very reluctant to change common modules - too often we copy
existing modules to avoid this work and risk - negating the advantages
of them.  

It is all part of the mindset which will need to be changed.
```

Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> Typically a mainframe programmer wants to do a search in the program he
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Typically, we work from the compile listing of the modules.
In a large system, it is impossible to have a single set of programs
that incorporate all of the system logic. Typically, bugs occur in the
data passed from one program to another. This is how we track down the
offending program. You can look at the program logic until you are blue
in the face and never discover this kind of bug. It requires trapping
the offending data and running experiments to backtrack where the error occurred.
> 
> Copybooks make it hard for programmers - but we have been sold on their
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
I find this hard to fathom in this day of PC's with their multiple
windows. I have been know to have up to 14 3270 sessions alive whilst
debugging code.
> 
> Also, mainframes often have very strict version control.  We have to
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> It is all part of the mindset which will need to be changed.
True, however, version control is not an excuse to not create or update
common modules. You need to implement a black box testing scheme where
you enter all the various inputs and certify that the resulting outputs
are correct. Then when you change the common modules, you run these
tests again to prove to the controllers that you have not broken anything.
check out HTTP://www.extremeprograming.org
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OO Has it Worked ? : Was: Multiple Topics - CA-World discussed too!

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2000-04-21T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38FFF78F.9709CEE3@home.com>`
- **References:** `<38fb651c.23035054@news.cox-internet.com> <vv1L4.3099$zG.2419679@news1.mco> <38FE79E6.6EE9@swbell.net>`

```


"Michael C. Kasten" wrote:
> 
> For those who have been actually using OO COBOL, rather than just
> toying with it, as I have done: Where has the OO paradigm worked?
> Where has it failed?  In future projects, where would you expect it to
> be most (and least) successful?

Well apart from Michael's note above there were many other comments in
subsequent messages, so I'll try and answer them as somebody who is
'using' OO as opposed to 'toying'.

First off, PC, flat files, single thread. Per Oil Corporation/Gas Plant
my volumes could hardly been considered big, let alone large. The system
is geared to handling one client/facility, but the end user can
chop-and-change at will.

So I can't speculate on the possibilities with mainframes. (Thinking
back to my systems days with mainframes, I'm guessing I might well stick
with structured for number crunching applications and I'd also have to
take into account those DBs which have been discussed here in some
detail). 

Let me re-state - time wise learning - OO = 20% and OO GUIs = 80%. Had I
been aware of something like Flexus SP2, we could forget about that 80%
- but that would probably also have meant I wouldn't have gotten into OO
as well.

Question 1 and 2 - "Where has the OO paradigm worked? Where has it
failed?"

Let's take the easy one first. It hasn't failed me at all. I can't think
of an instance (no pun intended), where it has let me down.

Where has it worked - sorry folks but that word 're-use' comes into
play. I know the same is achievable from well organized structured,
calling sub-routines, but I honestly feel OO gives a slight edge. It
starts from the business planning stage and I consider it essential that
you think three tier :-

	- Business Logic
	- User Interface ( I prefer Screen displays, but that doesn't 
	  allow for voice communication etc., does it)
	- DBI - in my case flat files, 90%+ being indexed.

(BTW : The term three tier isn't something I dreamed up. My first
pick-up on it was a reference made to it in M/F Compuserve Forum - guess
who - Pete Dashwood !).

For consistency all my code is OO-style, (i.e. classes) - so I think in
one style - I could quite happily intermix structured for business
logic. (Note for Howard - which Thane has already responded to - you can
mix structured with OO - invoking methods instead of calling
sub-routines - a candidate to try this on might be date routines).

Obviously there's nothing unique about the business logic, it changes
pattern depending upon whether you are doing an edit, calculations or
printing - no set format where OO is going to give you an edge over
structured. From mainframe days, I doubt there was sufficient similarity
between updating vendor transactions and updating general ledger
transactions, (what say you folks dealing in big volumes ?)

Now to the reusability - it has occurred in two areas primarily, files
and screens. 

Files - Following what I call the WillPrice method (objetz.com) I have a
pretty fire-proof system for all flat-file handling. Give me the record
format for your file and I'll have it up and running in 30 minutes. (Now
of course you've got to write programs that access that file - but the
file handling is written and compiled).

Another factor that combines with files is my use of
collections/dictionaries. This technique also allows me to get rid of
alternate keys to access records alphabetically by name or another key.

Screens (#$%%&## Windows !) Goes without saying GUIs were a pig. I
really want to concentrate on COBOL not Windoze. For the subset of
controls that I use and need, I have now created a template MyDialog in
the same fashion as a template for files. It creates a dialog,
regardless of content. Took a hell of while to get to this stage - but
having done it - it can serve as a basis for others to improve upon and
fine tune.

Now here is where I will fall foul of OO purists. I believe in treating
the screen as a 'dumb terminal' - it merely displays/accepts and makes
no validation checks. That is all done in business logic. This touches
on another comment somebody made, to the effect  :-

'...... ridiculous turning strings into objects.....'

COBOL is a data centric language - it just doesn't make sense to turn
data into objects to pass between one text handling class and another
text handling class. My references to objects ( object reference =
pointer) is to called classes, (particularly files) and turning data
into objects is restricted to two areas :-

- entries included in collections/dictionaries (i.e. open-ended W/S
tables). I use these for validity checking plus they are the guts for
filling both listboxes and droplists.

- Screens - MyDialog.cbl which receives data as text/numeric ( converts
to display objects) and returns entered data - entered objects (
returned to business logic as text/numeric).

Somebody commented about classes - although I use Merant support classes
indirectly I am not using a 1,001 methods to achieve the above. Consider
collections :-

(a) Creation -	Read file - format collection record - add to collection
(b) Add new collection record
(c) Change collection record
(d) Delete collection record

so I'm using very few methods, although there are probably at least some
200 methods that you could select from.

Keeping my screening to MyDialog - that too reduces the number of
methods I need - just one class needing to worry about GUIs.

Question 3 - In future projects, where would you expect it to
be most (and least) successful?

Assuming I'm still talking flat files and Windoze - then the file
handling and screen handling immediately comes into play. Just like any
approach, design a set of canned routines and you use them as templates
to create a new project.

As to "least successful" - can only speculate. My design philosophy is
to return each piece of data to business logic for storage or redisplay,
(converting input integers to displayable decimals). So this approach
doesn't sit happily with the 'Net approach of key it all in, press
<SEND> and then we'll tell you what you left out. So, getting into the
HTML jazz etc. I could run into problems - but that might merely mean
developing MyDialog-MkII.cbl.

I hope that if I need to go multi-user then I can easily adapt my file
handling by adding LOCK, UNLOCK etc.... Similarly I would hope that if
switching to a DB, I could create a file-handling class geared to the
DB.

The real key to all this, (hence my constant plugs for public-domain
source), - is that somebody has to give it a shot. It wont be perfect
and it wont necessarily be the most efficient way. Then along come
others, pore over the source, hack, re-assemble, fine-tune etc. and 
produce MkII, MkIII, MkIV etc......

Hope above represents a reasonable coverage.

Jimmy
```

generated vitriolic comments' :-)
 
Well I read your article and the replies. Shall I, shan't I ? What the
hell - I will comment. Firstly serves 'em right for using C and
certainly C++ !!!

I keep on saying it, OO is NOT a religion it is another methodology, and
I am quite happily heretical; if what I do in OO works but it is not
'pure' - what the hell.

As a COBOL compiler writer you don't need to be told of COBOL's
facilities, which allow you to mix 'n match :-

	- structured (Guys - don't let's get into a hooha again over
	  what that means !)
	- OO
	- structured and OO
	- COBOL flat files or DBs or both
	- Screening - Screen Section, tools such as SP2 or ScreenIO
	  to do windowing or separate GUI classes
	- plus others use Activex and Java
	- and sure they throw in C/C++ where it grabs their fancy.

Having quickly scanned the reply messages to your article, three that
stand out :-

- Compiles - I don't find them at all slow (is this because C etc. have
so many include files ?)

- Design - Without a doubt you need to give a lot of thought up front as
to what you want to do. That will change as source code models become
available in COBOL for others to scan for ideas. At the moment you've
got to think this one out for yourself - and I most certainly wouldn't
look at any of that C crap for guidance.

- Classes - coupled with design is the class syndrome. If you think OOP
is Nuts - this is an area where it can really go nuts. There is the
danger that you can go into overload with inheritance. At the moment my
approach is :-

	EditCustomer----->MyDialog(generic GUI displayer)
	.
	CustomerDBI(inherits from DBIMethods)
	.
	DBIMethods
	.
	CustomerFile

Most of my flat file accesses bypass CustomerDBI and go straight to
DBIMethods and then on to the appropriate file. 

Now I could get real cutesey and think about inheritance, polymorphism,
all the other nice jazz words and screw myself up royally in the
process. I've stuck to the above basic structure, and because
collections/dictionaries are generated as a result of what is in the
flat files, all my collections/dictionaries are assembled/changed in the
appropriate FileBDBI - and I don't give a bugger about 'hiding'.

As I use it, OO COBOL works for me - and definitely reusability comes
into play, carbon copying methods from one FileDBI to another. (Again I
could get cutesey and extract all the collection create/change methods 
from individual FileDBIs and put them into an 'assembly class' and
confuse the hell out of myself in the process).

Agreed much of what I've illustrated above can be paralleled in
'structured' with appropriate calls or entry points. However, I find the
OO approach a neat model for me and like working with it.

I like it, use it, and it works for me. You don't like OO, don't use it.
QED.

Jimmy, Calgary AB

"James J. Gavan" wrote:

> I like it, use it, and it works for me. You don't like OO, don't use it.
> QED.

Not that easy.  If I want to make programs this much different from what
other programmers in my shop are familiar with, I need to cost justify
this change.  If the shop decides to go OO, I go OO, if the shop decides
to wait and see how maintenance works in a large enterprise, I wait.

Nothing stops me from doing OO analysis though.  Nobody will maintain my
analysis, only the results of my analysis.

- **Message-ID:** `<8g1500$30kf$1@news.hitter.net>`

```
What occurs to me is: There is a flaw in the class design for
Register.

There are three types of registers: read-only (RO), write-only (WO),
and bi-directional (BD); but only one Register class. These
different register types have different behavior.

What is the effect of using the bit-wise operators on a RO or WO
register, or, more importantly, where both RO and WO registers
are assigned to the same address?

If using the bit-wise operators does not make sense, why have them
available? The effect of having one simple Register class is to defer
a coding error to run time when it could have been caught during
compilation.

As for OO COBOL, I remain somewhat pessimistic; but I am looking
at it.
------------------
Rick Smith
